Anita RØNNE

Associate Professor in Energy Law, 
University of Copenhagen

Anita Rønne is Associate Professor in Energy Law in the 
Faculty of Law, University of Copenhagen. She has been 
appointed  by  the  minister  as  a  Member  of  the  Danish 
Energy  Regulatory  Authority,  as  Chair  of  the  Valua-
tion  Committee  under  the  Law  of  Renewables,  and  as 
an  expert  to  the  Danish  Safety  Technology  Authority. 
She  holds  the  chair  of  the  Danish  Energy  Law  Society 
and is a Member and former Chairman of the Interna-
tional Bar Association’s (IBA) Academic Advisory Group 
within  the  Section  on  Energy,  Environment,  Natural 
Resources  and  Infrastructure  Law  (SEERIL).  She  is  also 
a Member of the Committee on Climate Change of the 
International  Law  Association,  and  of  the  study  board 

of  the  North  Sea  Energy  Law  Programme  (Universities 
of Oslo, Aberdeen and Groningen, funded by the EU — 
L.L.L.P). She teaches within the areas of energy law and 
climate change law. She has administrative experience 
from the Danish Ministry of Energy and as a consultant 
under European Commission and World Bank second-
ment  (eastern  Europe,  South  Africa,  Malaysia).  She  is 
an author and co-editor of Energy law in Europe; Energy 
security; and Regulating energy and natural resources (all 
Oxford University Press), Legal systems and wind energy 
(Kluwer Law International and DJØF Publishing) and has 
drafted many book chapters and articles (in English and 
Danish).

61

Towards a sustainable energy mix — 

new horizons and pitfalls for the legal regime

Anita RØNNE

Associate Professor in Energy Law, Law Faculty, University of Copenhagen;
Member of the Danish Energy Regulatory Authority;
Chair of the Danish Energy Law Society.

In the course of the last decades, the necessity to weigh 
up  the  possibilities  of  economic  growth  against  the 
environmental consequences has been increasingly rec-
ognised politically — on a national as well as an interna-
tional level. The result has been a diff erent order of prior-
ity with environmental protection and the preservation 
of natural resources higher on the list. To properly assess 
the energy mix debate, a number of considerations have 
to  be  taken  including  that  energy  demand  will  grow 
worldwide  and  fossil  fuel  resources  will  concentrate  in 
fewer states with the consequential risk to energy secu-
rity.

The  1992  United  Nations  Conference  on  Environment 
and  Development  in  Rio  de  Janeiro  emphasised  that 
states  have  the  sovereign  right  to  exploit  their  own 
resources pursuant to their own environmental policies, 
ensuring that the activities do not cause damage to the 
environment  of  other  states  or  of  common  areas  and 
within  a  sustainable  development  framework.  Twenty 
years  later,  the  concluding  document  ‘The  future  we 
want’,  agreed  at  the  Rio+20,  likewise  acknowledges  a 
sustainable  development  where  economic,  social  and 
environmental considerations play an equal role. Energy 
is recognised as having a critical role in global develop-
ment  and  a  sustainable  access  energy  model  needs  to 
be identifi ed and implemented across the globe where 
more  than  1.4  billion  people  are  still  without  electric-
ity.  A  proper  debate  on  energy  sustainability  includes 
careful  consideration  of  the  appropriate  energy  mix 
and  implies  a  paradigm  shift  in  energy  policy  towards 
a higher emphasis on renewable energy, effi  ciency and 
low-carbon societies. Addressing global warming is very 
much a matter of addressing energy use, as the use of 
fossil  fuels  is  the  dominant  anthropogenic  cause  of 
global  warming.  Realistically,  however,  traditional  and 
renewable resources will have to coexist in the short and 
medium  term.  The  Rio+20  outcomes  go  hand-in-hand 
with  the  UN  nomination  of  2012  as  the  ‘International 
year of sustainable energy for all’ (SE4All). This challenge 
needs to be connected to the redirection of 2.5 billion 
energy  users,  who  are  currently  using  only  traditional 

biomass for energy consumption, to more effi  cient and 
environmentally safer sources.

The EU and other major world economies like the US and 
China are investing heavily in clean technologies. There 
are  diff erent  pathways  to  decarbonisation  and  in  each 
country  the  choice  of  means  to  be  deployed  depends 
on a combination of policy decisions, economic condi-
tions and resources, public acceptance, and extant legal 
and  regulatory  frameworks.  The  new  European  energy 
strategy seeks to answer how the triple challenges and 
core objectives of energy policy could be met: sustain-
able development, competitiveness and security of sup-
ply, at least refl ecting that the overall energy imports for 
this region is close to 55 % and increasingly comes from 
politically unstable parts of the world. Renewables play 
a signifi cant role in this context, and even in a country 
like Denmark which is the only EU-27 Member State that 
covers  its  own  energy  demand,  radical  reforms  have 
been  introduced  with  a  current  reduction  of  fossil  fuel 
consumption  by  a  factor  of  20  %  and  the  aspirational 
goal to be fossil fuel free by 2050.

The  EU  has,  as  part  of  its  action  on  climate  change, 
agreed  on  a  reduction  of  greenhouse  gas  emissions 
by 20 % in 2020 combined with an indicative target to 
improve energy effi  ciency by 20 % and a binding Europe-
wide 20 % target for renewable energy. Experience with 
voluntary  targets  for  renewables  confi rms  that  public 
regulation is also necessary. The new Directive 2009/28/
EC on renewables is also intended to remove unneces-
sary barriers to the growth of renewable energy — for 
example  by  simplifying  the  administrative  procedures. 
The  energy  roadmap  2050  (December  2011)  indicates 
some  targets  such  as  increasing  renewable  energy 
consumption,  optimising  energy  consumption,  and 
strengthening the external dimension of the EU energy 
market without taking a position on nuclear power pro-
duction. In this context, however, it should be mentio-
ned that some countries like Japan, Germany and Swit-
zerland have announced their intention to end nuclear 
power production, but without releasing details on how 

62

to cover the energy gaps deriving from such a decision 
and  with  a  possible  unavoidable  increase  in  fossil  fuel 
use for energy purposes.

The  debate  on  energy  needs  to  pay  due  considera-
tion  to  a  complex  regulatory  frame  where  existing 
laws  regulating  the  energy  domain  are  fragmented  at 
diff erent levels and between diff erent  sectors. Discon-
nected  and  uncoordinated  regulatory  frames  with  an 
indirect impact on energy coexist today, e.g. in the EU 
on  trade,  environment,  energy,  transport  and  agricul-
ture. It is also a challenge that the principle of soverei-
gnty allows national decisions in the fi eld of energy that 
are becoming increasingly transnational in their scope. 
Developments  within  international  environmental  law 
indicate  that  the  notion  of  common  responsibility  is 
being  incorporated  with  a  view  to  responsibilities  for 
future generations and protection against damages to 
the environment of other states. The principle of sove-
reignty  is  therefore  coming  under  pressure  internatio-
nally as well as regionally.

EU law has already prepared some legal tools for com-
mon  EU  actions  in  the  energy  sector  and  for  environ-
ment  protection.  The  new  Treaty  on  the  Functioning 

of  the  European  Union  (TFEU),  as  amended  by  the 
Lisbon  Treaty,  confi rms  the  increasing  priority  of  the 
energy sector by including a special title (XXI) devoted 
to energy. It follows-up on a course that was initially set 
by the Treaty on European Union (the 1992 Maastricht 
Treaty) which added energy measures to the list of the 
Community’s  activities.  TFEU’s  Article  194  includes  the 
objectives  of  the  EU  energy  policy  and  advocates  soli-
darity in the fi elds of energy and a prudent utilisation of 
natural resources pursuant to Article 191. Now that the 
principle of solidarity has been introduced in the Treaty 
for the energy sector, it must be expected to play a role 
in particular concerning energy security issues at the EU 
level.  Although  the  Member  States  have  retained  their 
sovereignty over primary energy sources and choice of 
energy  mix,  it  is  beyond  doubt  that  the  EU  will  focus 
more  and  more  on  common  approaches  and  national 
subsidiarity will pay the price of this.

Fundamental  changes  are  taking  place  in  government 
regulation of the energy sector globally. Challenges and 
dilemmas coexist for science, policy, economy, law and 
ethics including paradigm shifts that need to take into 
account  the  long-term  horizons,  globalisation  and  the 
schism between the developed and developing world.

74

EGE round table 
on the energy mix in Europe

Pier VELLINGA

Wageningen University, Vrij Universiteit (VU) 
Amsterdam, Chairman of the national research 
programme ‘Knowledge for Climate’

Pier  Vellinga  is  Professor  in  Environmental  Sciences 
and  Climate  Change  at  Wageningen  University  and 
Research  Centre  and  VU  University  Amsterdam.  Origi-
nally he specialised in coastal engineering, contributing 
to the ‘Deltaplan’ in the Netherlands. In 1987 he became 
advisor to the Dutch Environment Minister for Climate 
Change. In that period he helped to shape international 
climate policies and he was one of the initiators and fi rst 
bureau  members  of  the  Intergovernmental  Panel  on 

Climate Change. In 1991 he joined the Vrije Universiteit 
as Professor in Environmental Sciences and as Director 
of  the  Institute  for  Environmental  Studies.  In  2007  he 
also  took  up  a  position  at  Wageningen  University  and 
Research  Centre  to  lead  the  national  climate  change 
research programme ‘Knowledge for climate’. Pier Vel-
linga  is  a  board  member  of  several  research  institutes 
and  environmental  organisations  in  the  Netherlands 
and abroad.
